So Attack 6 would lower mean slightly, but query day's mean is actually higher than the reference Day 4's mean (36.41 vs 36.90). That doesn't fit because capping high values would lower mean, not raise it. So Attack 6 is unlikely.

Now Attack 7 (subtract constant) would shift all values down, reducing mean by about the constant. If constant ~1.06, then mean would drop from something like 37.96 (if normal mean was ~37.96) to 36.90. But the reference week's overall mean across days is about 36.6-37.1. So subtracting a constant would make the mean much lower than typical, but our query day mean 36.90 is not lower than typical; it's within the range of normal means. Actually Day 1 mean 37.01, Day 6 mean 37.13, Day 0 36.96. So a normal day could have mean 36.90. So it's possible that the original day (which was manipulated) had a higher mean, say 38.0, and after subtraction became 36.9, which coincidentally looks like a normal mean. That could be the attacker trying to make the day look like a normal day, using Attack 7 to reduce consumption but still appear plausible. However, the attacker might subtract just enough to keep mean in normal range. But the defining characteristic is that all readings are uniformly reduced, so the relative differences remain but absolute values are lower, potentially making the max lower than normal because the original max was higher. Since the query day's max 39.07 is lower than the typical max 40.13, it fits the subtraction hypothesis: if original max was 40.13, subtract 1.06 gives 39.07. That works. The min might also be lower if original min was 27.46 (or 26.40). If original min was 26.40, after subtraction it would be 25.34, which is lower than 26.40. Since query day min is 26.40, that suggests the original min was higher, maybe 27.46, which is common. So the subtraction could have turned 27.46 into 26.40. So that matches. Also, note that many readings in the query day are 36.96, 38.01, 39.07, which are exactly one step down from the common normal values 38.01, 39.07, 40.13. So Attack 7 with offset ~1.06 is a strong candidate.

Now check Attack 4 (reduction over randomly selected period). Could be that only a portion of the day was reduced. But we see consistently lowered high values throughout the day (no 40.13 at all). The pattern is entire day without 40.13. Could be a partial reduction, but it's less plausible because 40.13 is missing everywhere.
Attack 9 (flat constant) not present.
Attack 10 (reversal) not.
Attack 12 (using another user's pattern) could produce a different pattern; but query day looks similar to the customer's own pattern with a shift.
Attack IEEE: random zeroing, no zeros here.

Thus, most likely Attack 7.

Confidence: High, because the pattern is a uniform downward shift preserving shape and the max is exactly one increment lower than normal max, while mean remains plausible.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Output Format:
Classification:
- Original | Manipulated

Identified Attack:  (fill only if manipulated)
- Most Likely: Attack X
- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:  (fill only if manipulated)
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week
- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes

The main concern: Are there any signs of specific attack patterns? Let's check the list of attacks and their characteristics:

Attack 0: all zeros. Query day is not all zeros.
Attack 1: multiply by constant factor between 0 and 1. Would preserve trend exactly but scale down magnitude. The ratio of query to reference might be constant? Not exactly constant across all times. Check some points: At 18:00, reference 702.87, query 662.53, ratio ~0.943. At 5:00, reference 330.49, query 303.34, ratio ~0.918. At noon? That could produce a pattern like this: a scaled version but with random variation. Also Attack 1 is constant factor, Attack 4 is random period reduction, Attack 5 replaces with average times random, Attack 6 cut-off, Attack 7 subtract cut-off, Attack 9 flat average, Attack 10 reverse, Attack 12 replace with other user.

So the plausible attacks are those that reduce magnitude but keep trend, possibly with varying factors: Attack 3 (multiply by different random factors per sample) or Attack 4 (reduce over a randomly selected period). Attack_Behavior_Explanation:

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: The attacker reduces measurements exclusively during the daytime high-consumption period (roughly morning to evening), while leaving nighttime (low‑usage) readings untouched. The reduction appears to be proportional, dampening peaks by about 10–20% without introducing noise or flat ceilings.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is lowered—maximum and mean consumption are smaller, while the minimum remains similar. The trend is altered: the daily cycle is still visible but becomes less pronounced (flatter) because the gap between peak and base shrinks; standard deviation decreases.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning content

We need to compare the query day's consumption pattern with the reference week. The reference week shows consistent patterns: typical daily profiles with low consumption at night, a morning ramp-up, peak around mid-day, and evening decline. The per-day statistics for the reference week show means around 530-574, max around 770-803, min around 270-307. The query day statistics: min=278.55, max=699.50, std=131.15, mean=508.72.

So the query day has overall reduced consumption magnitude, especially in the peak hours, but it's not completely zeroed or flat.

- Attack 1 (constant scaling): ruled out because night values are nearly identical to normal, consistent scaling would also reduce night values proportionally. Thus reasoning provided.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation for Attack 4: 
- How manipulated: Energy theft over a selected period (the high-usage daytime hours). The attacker likely reduces reported consumption during peak hours, possibly by bypassing the meter or injecting false data only when consumption is high. In this case, the nighttime values remain unaltered, while daytime values are decreased, resulting in a profile that looks normal at night but with suppressed peaks during the day. The reduction factor appears to vary but generally scales down the measurements (e.g., by around 10-20% or by a dynamic factor).
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The magnitude is reduced, especially the maximum and mean. The trend is altered because the daytime peaks are less pronounced relative to the nighttime base, making the daily pattern flatter (though still visible). The standard deviation decreases.
- Possible technical causes: Meter bypass during high-usage periods (e.g., using a switch to bypass the meter when consumption is high), magnetic interference that saturates only during higher currents, reverse current injection, or false data injection targeting specific time windows.

Thus final answer.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: The high‑consumption afternoon/evening block of the normal Sunday is moved to the early morning and mid‑morning periods, while the historically low early morning block is shifted to the later part of the day. This effectively reverses the load profile.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning: The query day shows a reversed pattern: the low consumption period seen in the reference week's early morning is replaced with higher consumption, while the high consumption period of the afternoon/evening is lower. Classification:
- Manipulated

Identified_Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 1
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day's consumption profile closely follows the shape and pattern of the reference week’s typical behavior (e.g., low overnight values, morning ramp-up, daytime peaks, evening decline), but all values are uniformly reduced. The mean drops from 67.41 (reference Day 4) to 58.26 (query), a reduction of ~13.5%, and peak values are similarly scaled (max from 100.50 to 89.65). This consistent, multiplicative reduction across the entire day strongly indicates a constant scaling factor. Attack 3 (random per‑sample scaling) would introduce erratic fluctuations that disrupt the trend, but the query day maintains smooth transitions. Attack 4 (partial period reduction) would show normal consumption in some intervals and reduced in others, which is not observed. Attack 6/7 (capping) would produce flat‑topped or zeroed segments, absent here.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: Each actual consumption sample is multiplied by a single constant random factor between 0 and 1 (estimated ~0.85–0.90). All readings are reduced proportionally, so the overall demand pattern remains intact but is shifted downward in magnitude.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is uniformly decreased; the daily mean, maximum, and minimum are all lower than those of the reference week. The trend (temporal shape) is fully preserved because the scaling factor is constant over the day.
- Possible technical causes: Meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, or false data injection.

You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

The overall shape is similar but query day seems to have lower peaks and lower overall consumption. The evening drop-off is similar. But the query day has a strange pattern: after the high evening peak (index 67: 89.65), it drops to lower values, but then around index 80-88, it goes down to 55.24, 51.82, then suddenly 35.87, 38.14, 35.30, 36.44, 37.00, 33.59, 33.59, 34.16. That seems like a normal late-night drop, similar to reference.

However, the query day has a mean 58.26 vs reference day 4 mean 67.41, a reduction of about 13.5%. The max is also reduced. The shape is largely similar but compressed. This could be an Attack 1 (multiplied by constant random factor between 0 and 1). Attack 1 multiplies all samples by a constant factor less than 1, so the trend remains the same, magnitude reduced. That matches: the overall daily pattern is maintained (morning low, rise, evening decline) but the values are lower. For example, the reference day morning low averages around 35-40, query morning low averages around 33-37, close but slightly lower. The midday peaks in reference are often 90-100, query peaks rarely exceed 85 (max 89.65 at one point). It seems like a uniform scaling. Let's test by computing ratio of query to reference for some matching times. But we need to be careful: the query day is not exactly the same date, so consumption might vary naturally. But the reduction seems consistent. Could it be Attack 3 (multiplied by different random factors per sample)? The difference between Attack 1 and Attack 3 is that Attack 1 uses a constant factor for all samples, Attack 3 uses different factors per sample. That would change the trend more noticeably, causing erratic fluctuations. In the query day, the trend looks smooth and similar to the reference day shape. So Attack 3 would likely produce a noisier pattern. Attack 4 is reduction over a random period; that would show a specific interval with reduced values and others normal. Here, the entire day seems reduced. Attack 5 replaces with false values from mean * random; that would completely destroy pattern. Attack 6 and 7 cap values, which would cause flat tops at a threshold. In query day, there is no visible cap; values vary. Attack 9 constant average, not seen. Attack 10 reverses pattern, not seen. The mean ratio 58.26/67.41 ≈ 0.864. The max ratio 89.65/100.50 ≈ 0.892. So fairly consistent. Attack 1 definition: multiplies actual consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). So factor ~0.86. That fits.

We should rule out other attacks: Attack 7 subtracts a cutoff; that would reduce values but also produce zeros if subtraction exceeds original. Here no zeros. Attack 2 zeros for random duration; no zeros. Attack 0 all zeros; no. Attack 6 cap, no flat tops. Attack 12 would replace with another user's pattern, but the pattern looks like the same user's pattern scaled down. So Attack 1 is best.

Therefore, classification: Manipulated. Identified attack: Attack 1. Confidence: High, because the consumption profile preserves its shape but is uniformly lower, consistent with a scaling attack, and no other attack type matches the observed pattern.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.
